<p>The <b>Wii U</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Àå/ secondary stress follows">Àå</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/iÀê/ long 'e' in 'seed'">iÀê</span></span> <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Àà/ primary stress follows">Àà</span><span title="/juÀê/ long 'u' in 'cute'">juÀê</span></span>/</a></span></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">WEE</span></span></b></i></a></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">YOO</span></span></b></i></a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Home_video_game_console" title="Home video game console">home video game console</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Nintendo" title="Nintendo">Nintendo</a>, and the successor to the <a href="/wiki/Wii" title="Wii">Wii</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-codename_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-codename-6">[6]</a></sup> The console was released in November 2012<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pricerelease_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-pricerelease-8">[8]</a></sup> and was the first <a href="/wiki/Eighth_generation_of_video_game_consoles" title="Eighth generation of video game consoles">eighth-generation video game console</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> as it competes with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sony_Computer_Entertainment" title="Sony Computer Entertainment">Sony</a>'s <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_4" title="PlayStation 4">PlayStation 4</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Xbox_One" title="Xbox One">Xbox One</a>.</p>
<p>The Wii U is the first Nintendo console to support <a href="/wiki/High-definition_video" title="High-definition video">HD</a> <a href="/wiki/Video_game_graphics" title="Video game graphics">graphics</a>. The system's primary <a href="/wiki/Gamepad" title="Gamepad">controller</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Wii_U_GamePad" title="Wii U GamePad">Wii U GamePad</a>, which features an embedded <a href="/wiki/Touchscreen" title="Touchscreen">touchscreen</a>, and combines directional buttons, analog sticks, and action buttons. The screen can be used either as a supplement to the main display (either providing an alternate, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/asymmetric" title="wikt:asymmetric">asymmetric</a> gameplay experience, or a means of local multiplayer without resorting to a <a href="/wiki/Split_screen_(computer_graphics)" title="Split screen (computer graphics)">split screen</a>), or in <a href="/wiki/Off-TV_Play" title="Off-TV Play">supported games</a>, to play the game directly on the GamePad independently of the television. The Wii U is <a href="/wiki/Backward_compatibility" title="Backward compatibility">backward compatible</a> with all Wii software and accessories ‚Äì games can support any combination of the GamePad, <a href="/wiki/Wii_Remote" title="Wii Remote">Wii Remote</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wii_Nunchuk" title="Wii Nunchuk">Nunchuk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wii_Balance_Board" title="Wii Balance Board">Balance Board</a>, or Nintendo's more traditionally designed <a href="/wiki/Classic_Controller" title="Classic Controller">Classic Controller</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wii_U_Pro_Controller" title="Wii U Pro Controller">Wii U Pro Controller</a> for input. Online functionality centers around the <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Network" title="Nintendo Network">Nintendo Network</a> platform and <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Network#Miiverse" title="Nintendo Network">Miiverse</a>, an integrated <a href="/wiki/Social_networking_service" title="Social networking service">social networking service</a> which allows users to share content in game-specific communities.</p>
<p>The Wii U was met with a generally positive reception; praising its GamePad controller, improvements to online functionality over the Wii, backwards compatibility with existing Wii software and controllers, affordability in comparison to other eighth-generation consoles, and non-reliance on a subscription for online functionality, while criticizing the GamePad's battery life and issues with the console's user interface and functionality, along with a weak lineup of launch titles and a lack of clear goals. The Wii U was met with slow consumer adoption, with low sales primarily credited to a weak lineup of <a href="/wiki/Launch_title" title="Launch title">launch titles</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> and third-party support,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> and marketing reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In March 2017 Nintendo will release a new flagship home console, <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Switch" title="Nintendo Switch">Nintendo Switch</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h3>
<p>The system was first conceived in 2008,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IwataAsksWiiU1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-IwataAsksWiiU1-14">[14]</a></sup> after Nintendo recognized several limitations and challenges with the <a href="/wiki/Wii" title="Wii">Wii</a>, such as the general public's perception that the system catered primarily to a "<a href="/wiki/Casual_game" title="Casual game">casual</a>" audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IwataAsksWiiU2_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-IwataAsksWiiU2-15">[15]</a></sup> With Wii U, Nintendo wished to bring back "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_gamer" title="Core gamer">core</a>" gamers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IwataAsksWiiU3_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-IwataAsksWiiU3-16">[16]</a></sup> Game designer <a href="/wiki/Shigeru_Miyamoto" title="Shigeru Miyamoto">Shigeru Miyamoto</a> admitted that the lack of HD and limited network infrastructure for Wii also contributed to the system being regarded in a separate class to its competitors' systems, the <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_3" title="PlayStation 3">PlayStation 3</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xbox_360" title="Xbox 360">Xbox 360</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IwataAsksWiiU4_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-IwataAsksWiiU4-17">[17]</a></sup> It was decided that a new console would have to be developed to accommodate significant structural changes. Ideas on which direction to take for the new console led to a lot of debate within the company, and the project started over from scratch on several occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IwataAsksWiiU5_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-IwataAsksWiiU5-18">[18]</a></sup> The concept of a touchscreen embedded within the controller was originally inspired by the blue light on the Wii disc tray that illuminates to indicate new messages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IwataAsksWiiU6_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-IwataAsksWiiU6-19">[19]</a></sup> Miyamoto and his team wanted to include a small screen to provide game feedback and status messages to players (similar to the <a href="/wiki/VMU" title="VMU">VMU</a> for <a href="/wiki/Sega" title="Sega">Sega</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Dreamcast" title="Dreamcast">Dreamcast</a>). Much later in development, this was expanded to a full screen that could display the game being played in its entirety, a concept which was suggested but not financially viable earlier in the project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IwataAsksWiiU6_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-IwataAsksWiiU6-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Public rumors surrounding the console began to appear in 2010, with speculation of an upcoming revision of the Wii scheduled for 2011 known as the "Wii HD", that would support high definition video and have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blu-ray_disc" title="Blu-ray disc">Blu-ray disc</a> drive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> However, Nintendo president <a href="/wiki/Satoru_Iwata" title="Satoru Iwata">Satoru Iwata</a> later stated that he saw "no significant reason" to include HD into the Wii and that such an addition would be better suited for a successor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Miyamoto also expressed Nintendo's interest in working with HD graphics, but clarified that the company is primarily focused on gameplay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In October 2009, Miyamoto said that Nintendo had no concrete plans about a successor, but knew that the successor would possibly still feature motion controls and they expected its interface to be "more compact" and cheaper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Iwata also mentioned that the Wii's successor might be 3D-compatible but concluded that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rate_of_adoption" title="Rate of adoption">adoption rates</a> of <a href="/wiki/3D_television" title="3D television">3D televisions</a> should increase to at least 30% first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> In 2010, Nintendo of America president <a href="/wiki/Reggie_Fils-Aim%C3%A9" title="Reggie Fils-Aim√©">Reggie Fils-Aim√©</a> commented that he felt "confident the Wii home entertainment console has a very long life in front of it" and declared that a successor would not be launched in the near future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>After its <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Entertainment_Expo_2010" title="Electronic Entertainment Expo 2010">E3 2010</a> presentation, Iwata revealed to the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> that they would begin announcing a new console once Nintendo ran "out of ideas with the current hardware and cannot give users any more meaningful surprises with the technology".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Later, at an investor's meeting, he disclosed that they were "of course studying and developing the next console to Wii", but they were simultaneously keeping its concepts secret because it was "really important for [his] business to positively surprise people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Fils-Aim√© also stated that Nintendo's next home console would likely not feature <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopy" title="Stereoscopy">stereoscopic 3D</a>, based on the 3D technology Nintendo had experimented with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2011, an uncredited source indicated that Nintendo was planning to unveil a successor to the Wii known as "Project Caf√©" at its <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Entertainment_Expo_2011" title="Electronic Entertainment Expo 2011">E3 2011</a> presentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-codename_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-codename-6">[6]</a></sup> Caf√© was claimed to be a high definition console, also would have backward compatibility with Wii software.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nintendo_comment_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nintendo_comment-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-High-resolution_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-High-resolution-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reveal_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reveal-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kotaku_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kotaku-34">[34]</a></sup> Conflicting reports also surrounded the console's new controller, with reports suggesting a <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">tablet</a>-like device with an embedded touchcreen and the ability to stream games from the console directly to the screen, while others reported that the controller would be similar to the <a href="/wiki/GameCube_controller" title="GameCube controller">GameCube controller</a> and feature dual analog sticks, shoulder buttons, and triggers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EDGE_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-EDGE-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-01net_Translated_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-01net_Translated-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IGN_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-IGN-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Announcement">Announcement</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px">
<div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Wii U debut at <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Entertainment_Expo_2011" title="Electronic Entertainment Expo 2011">E3 2011</a></div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:E3_2011_-_Nintendo_girl_holding_the_Wii_U_controller_(5831893934).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/E3_2011_-_Nintendo_girl_holding_the_Wii_U_controller_%285831893934%29.jpg/250px-E3_2011_-_Nintendo_girl_holding_the_Wii_U_controller_%285831893934%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/E3_2011_-_Nintendo_girl_holding_the_Wii_U_controller_%285831893934%29.jpg/375px-E3_2011_-_Nintendo_girl_holding_the_Wii_U_controller_%285831893934%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/E3_2011_-_Nintendo_girl_holding_the_Wii_U_controller_%285831893934%29.jpg/500px-E3_2011_-_Nintendo_girl_holding_the_Wii_U_controller_%285831893934%29.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The <a href="#Wii_U_GamePad">controller</a> prototype originally shown at E3 2011.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On April 25, 2011, Nintendo released an official statement announcing a system to succeed the Wii to be released during 2012, and that playable console units would be present at <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Entertainment_Expo_2011" title="Electronic Entertainment Expo 2011">E3 2011</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Speaking at an investor's conference, Iwata stated the Wii successor would "offer something new for home game systems."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> He also confirmed that the device would not launch in fiscal year 2012, meaning that it would release after April 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> In early June, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nihon_Keizai_Shimbun" title="Nihon Keizai Shimbun">Nikkei</a> issued a report confirming earlier rumors that the new console would feature a controller with a 6¬†inch touchscreen that would give tablet-like controls to games, as well as a rechargeable battery and camera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Project Caf√© was officially unveiled as the Wii U during Nintendo's press conference at E3 2011 by Nintendo of America president <a href="/wiki/Reggie_Fils-Aim%C3%A9" title="Reggie Fils-Aim√©">Reggie Fils-Aim√©</a>; he explained that the Wii U would be "a system we will all enjoy together, but also one that's tailor-made for you"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polygon-stillhurting_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-polygon-stillhurting-43">[43]</a></sup> The rumored tablet controller was also shown at this time. No first-party titles for Wii U were officially announced during the presentation, but several major third-party studios announced planned titles for Wii U in a pre-recorded video, the possibility of a new <i><a href="/wiki/Super_Smash_Bros." title="Super Smash Bros.">Super Smash Bros.</a></i> game was teased, and <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Arts" title="Electronic Arts">Electronic Arts</a>' then-CEO <a href="/wiki/John_Riccitiello" title="John Riccitiello">John Riccitiello</a> appeared on-stage to discuss the company's prospective plans for products on Wii U. Prototype hardware and a series of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tech_demo" title="Tech demo">tech demos</a> were available to attendees, showcasing gameplay concepts and the technological capabilities of the console‚Äîincluding a <i><a href="/wiki/The_Legend_of_Zelda" title="The Legend of Zelda">The Legend of Zelda</a></i>-themed graphics demo, and <i><a href="/wiki/New_Super_Mario_Bros._U" title="New Super Mario Bros. U">New Super Mario Bros. Mii</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamespot-wiiuunveile3_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamespot-wiiuunveile3-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the two days following the unveiling of the Wii U, Nintendo's stock fell nearly 10 percent to levels not seen since 2006. Some analysts expressed skepticism in regards to the addition of a touch screen, expressing concern that the controller would be less affordable and less innovative than the original Wii Remote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gibiz-5yearlow_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-gibiz-5yearlow-47">[47]</a></sup> When asked about whether or not the Wii U was going to support stereographic 3D via <a href="/wiki/3D_television" title="3D television">3D televisions</a>, Iwata stated that it was "not the area we are focusing on."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WiiU3D_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-WiiU3D-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 26, 2012, Iwata announced that the Wii U would be launched by the end of the 2012 shopping season in all major regions, and that its final specifications would be revealed at E3 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nintendo.co.jp_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-nintendo.co.jp-49">[49]</a></sup> He also stated that the console would feature a unified online system known as <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Network" title="Nintendo Network">Nintendo Network</a>, which would feature user account support as opposed to the use of friend codes. Nintendo Network would also provide the framework for online multiplayer interactions, add-on content, as well as online distribution of applications and video games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Iwata mentioned that the Wii U GamePad would support <a href="/wiki/Near_field_communication" title="Near field communication">NFC</a>, which would allow the system to wirelessly interact with figurines and cards. It would also allow for <a href="/wiki/Virtual_goods" title="Virtual goods">microtransactions</a> to take place wirelessly using credit cards with NFC support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nintendo.co.jp_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-nintendo.co.jp-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 13, 2012, Nintendo announced that the Wii U would launch in Japan on December 8, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Later that day, Nintendo announced that the North American launch date would be November 18, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Nintendo of Europe and <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Australia" title="Nintendo Australia">Nintendo Australia</a> also announced that the Wii U would be released in both regions on November 30, 2012. In an interview with <i><a href="/wiki/GameSpot" title="GameSpot">GameSpot</a></i> the next day, Nintendo of America's Senior Product Marketing Manager <a href="/wiki/Bill_Trinen" title="Bill Trinen">Bill Trinen</a> admitted that Nintendo's pre-launch marketing and presentations of the Wii U had focused too much on the GamePad, to the extent that some consumers mistook the device as an accessory for the existing Wii rather than an entirely new platform. Trinen ensured that future promotional material for the console, including its packaging, would emphasize both the Wii U console and GamePad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamespot-messaging_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamespot-messaging-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release">Release</span></h2>
<p>The Wii U was originally released in two bundles: the Basic bundle and the Deluxe <small>(US)</small> / Premium <small>(WW)</small> bundle. The Basic bundle contains a white Wii U with 8¬†GB of storage, a white <a href="/wiki/Wii_U_GamePad" title="Wii U GamePad">Wii U GamePad</a> and stylus and an HDMI cable, while the Deluxe <small>(US)</small> / Premium <small>(WW)</small> contains a black Wii U with 32¬†GB of storage, a black GamePad and stylus, an HDMI cable, and adds a <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Network#Nintendo_Network_Premium" title="Nintendo Network">Nintendo Network Premium</a> subscription, the <i><a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Land" title="Nintendo Land">Nintendo Land</a></i> game (except Japan), as well as stands for the console and controller and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wii_Sensor_Bar" title="Wii Sensor Bar">sensor bar</a> (except Japan).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-wired-55">[55]</a></sup> The Wii U launched in North America priced at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US$" title="US$">US$</a>299.99 for the Basic Set and US$349.99 for the Deluxe Set. The system was scheduled to launch on November 25, 2012 in Mexico, however, it was delayed to November 29.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mexico_launch_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-mexico_launch-56">[56]</a></sup> The system launched in Europe, Australia and South Africa, with European pricing set by retailers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-south_africa_launch_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-south_africa_launch-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The system launched in Japan priced at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C2%A5" title="¬•">¬•</a>26,250 for the Basic Set and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C2%A5" title="¬•">¬•</a>31,500 for the Premium Set.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retail_configurations">Retail configurations</span></h3>
<p>On July 13, 2013, Nintendo released a white version of the Premium Pack in Japan, as well as an official battery pack and charger dock for the Wii Remote. The battery pack is capable of lasting 3 hours of gameplay before needing to be recharged. On July 25, the company released an improved battery pack for the Wii U GamePad. In contrast to the standard 1500mAh battery bundled with the console, the new 2550mAh battery pack increases longevity to between five and eight hours before needing to be recharged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>On August 28, 2013, Nintendo announced that the Deluxe <small>(US)</small> / Premium <small>(WW)</small> 32 GB model would drop in price from US$349.99 to US$299.99 in North America. The price drop took effect on September 20, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In Europe, Nintendo didn't confirm a formal price cut since individual retailers set their own prices. However, starting October 4, 2013, the company reduced the wholesale price of the system to retailers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_price_cut_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-EU_price_cut-61">[61]</a></sup> Coinciding with the system's price cut, Nintendo released a limited edition <i><a href="/wiki/The_Legend_of_Zelda:_The_Wind_Waker_HD" title="The Legend of Zelda: The Wind Waker HD">The Legend of Zelda: The Wind Waker HD</a></i> Wii U Deluxe Set bundle. The bundle included a black Wii U console with 32¬†GB of storage, a black Wii U GamePad decorated with a golden <a href="/wiki/Triforce" title="Triforce">Triforce</a> and other thematic symbols, a download code for the game and, in North America, a digital copy of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Legend_of_Zelda:_Hyrule_Historia" title="The Legend of Zelda: Hyrule Historia">The Legend of Zelda: Hyrule Historia</a></i>, a collector's book about <i><a href="/wiki/The_Legend_of_Zelda" title="The Legend of Zelda">The Legend of Zelda</a></i> series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_price_cut_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-EU_price_cut-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In Europe, Nintendo also released a limited edition <i><a href="/wiki/Lego_City_Undercover" title="Lego City Undercover">Lego City Undercover</a></i> Wii U Premium Pack bundle. Both European bundles featured a 7-day trial of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wii_Karaoke_U" title="Wii Karaoke U">Wii Karaoke U</a></i> service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_price_cut_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-EU_price_cut-61">[61]</a></sup> Nintendo did not cut the price of the Wii U in Australia or New Zealand nor did it release any of the previous bundles in the regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 31, 2013, Nintendo introduced two new Wii U Premium Pack bundles in Japan, called Family Set. The first included either a black or white Wii U console with 32¬†GB of storage, a black or white Wii U GamePad, <i><a href="/wiki/New_Super_Mario_Bros._U" title="New Super Mario Bros. U">New Super Mario Bros. U</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Wii_Party_U" title="Wii Party U">Wii Party U</a></i> preloaded, a black or white <a href="/wiki/Wii_Remote" title="Wii Remote">Wii Remote</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wii_Sensor_Bar" title="Wii Sensor Bar">Wii Sensor Bar</a>, and a 30-day trial of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wii_Karaoke_U" title="Wii Karaoke U">Wii Karaoke U</a></i> service. The second bundle included all of the previous one's contents, additionally preloaded with <i><a href="/wiki/Wii_Fit_U" title="Wii Fit U">Wii Fit U</a></i> and including Nintendo's official Fit Meter <a href="/wiki/Pedometer" title="Pedometer">pedometer</a> (with the <a href="/wiki/Wii_Balance_Board" title="Wii Balance Board">Wii Balance Board</a> available separately).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 1, 2013, Nintendo released a Mario &amp; Luigi Deluxe Set in North America with the intention of replacing the original Wii U Deluxe Set, which included a copy of <i><a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Land" title="Nintendo Land">Nintendo Land</a></i>. The Mario &amp; Luigi bundle contains both <i><a href="/wiki/New_Super_Mario_Bros._U" title="New Super Mario Bros. U">New Super Mario Bros. U</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/New_Super_Luigi_U" title="New Super Luigi U">New Super Luigi U</a></i> packaged as a "2 in 1" disc alongside a black Wii U console with 32¬†GB of storage and black Wii U GamePad controller.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The bundle was later released in Europe on November 8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_bundles_2013_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-EU_bundles_2013-67">[67]</a></sup> On November 14, Nintendo released a <i><a href="/wiki/Just_Dance_2014" title="Just Dance 2014">Just Dance 2014</a></i> Basic Pack bundle in Australia and New Zealand. It contains a white Wii U console with 8¬†GB of storage, a white Wii U GamePad and Wii Remote Plus controllers, a Sensor Bar, and disc versions of both <a href="/wiki/Ubisoft" title="Ubisoft">Ubisoft</a>'s <i>Just Dance 2014</i> and <i>Nintendo Land</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AUS_bundles_2013_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-AUS_bundles_2013-68">[68]</a></sup> The bundle was later released in Europe on November 22.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_bundles_2013_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-EU_bundles_2013-67">[67]</a></sup> On November 15, Nintendo released a <i><a href="/wiki/Wii_Party_U" title="Wii Party U">Wii Party U</a></i> Wii U Basic Pack bundle in Europe. It features a white Wii U console with 8¬†GB of storage, a white Wii U GamePad and Wii Remote Plus controllers, a Sensor Bar, and disc versions of both <i>Wii Party U</i> and <i>Nintendo Land</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_bundles_2013_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-EU_bundles_2013-67">[67]</a></sup> On November 15, Nintendo also released a <i><a href="/wiki/Skylanders:_Swap_Force" title="Skylanders: Swap Force">Skylanders: Swap Force</a></i> Wii U Basic Set bundle in North America. It contains white Wii U with 8¬†GB of storage, <a href="/wiki/Activision" title="Activision">Activision</a>'s <i>Skylanders Swap Force</i> game, a Portal of Power, three Skylanders figures, a collector poster, trading cards and sticker sheets, and a <i>Nintendo Land</i> game disc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The bundle was released in Australia and New Zealand on November 21.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AUS_bundles_2013_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-AUS_bundles_2013-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 26, 2013, the Wii U was released in Brazil. However, the system is only available in the black Deluxe Set in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brazil_launch_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-brazil_launch-70">[70]</a></sup> On January 10, 2015, Nintendo announced that it would cease selling consoles and games in Brazil due to the <a href="/wiki/Brazil_cost" title="Brazil cost">high cost of doing business in the country</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polygon-nintendobrazil_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-polygon-nintendobrazil-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>On May 30, 2014, Nintendo released a <i><a href="/wiki/Mario_Kart_8" title="Mario Kart 8">Mario Kart 8</a></i> Deluxe <small>(US)</small> / Premium <small>(WW)</small> Set bundle in Europe and North America. It features a black Wii U console with 32¬†GB of storage, a black Wii U GamePad, a copy of <i>Mario Kart 8</i>, a Sensor Bar, and special edition red <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wii_Wheel" title="Wii Wheel">Wii Wheel</a> (North America only). Additionally, registering the game on <a href="/wiki/Club_Nintendo" title="Club Nintendo">Club Nintendo</a> before July 31, 2014 presents the buyer a free Wii U game from a selection of four in North America and ten in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_Mario_Kart_8_bundle_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-EU_Mario_Kart_8_bundle-73">[73]</a></sup> The bundle was released in Australia and New Zealand on May 31.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AUS_Mario_Kart_8_bundle_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-AUS_Mario_Kart_8_bundle-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2015, the basic Wii U was discontinued in Japan, and replaced by a 32¬†GB "Premium" set that includes white hardware and a Wii Remote Plus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hardware">Hardware</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Espresso_(microprocessor)" title="Espresso (microprocessor)">Espresso (microprocessor)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nintendo-Wii-U-MCM.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1488" data-file-width="1488" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Nintendo-Wii-U-MCM.jpg/220px-Nintendo-Wii-U-MCM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Nintendo-Wii-U-MCM.jpg/330px-Nintendo-Wii-U-MCM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Nintendo-Wii-U-MCM.jpg/440px-Nintendo-Wii-U-MCM.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nintendo-Wii-U-MCM.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An illustration of Wii U's <a href="/wiki/Multi-chip_module" title="Multi-chip module">MCM</a> without heat spreader. The smaller chip, lower right, is the "<a href="/wiki/Espresso_(microprocessor)" title="Espresso (microprocessor)">Espresso</a>" CPU made by IBM. The other chips are the "Latte" GPGPU (large chip) from AMD and an <a href="/wiki/EEPROM" title="EEPROM">EEPROM</a> chip (tiny) from Renesas.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Wii U uses a custom <a href="/wiki/Multi-chip_module" title="Multi-chip module">multi-chip module</a> (MCM) developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AMD" title="AMD">AMD</a>, <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renesas" title="Renesas">Renesas</a> in co-operation with <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Integrated_Research_%26_Development" title="Nintendo Integrated Research &amp; Development">Nintendo IRD</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nintendo_Technology_Development" title="Nintendo Technology Development">Nintendo Technology Development</a>. The MCM combines an "<a href="/wiki/Espresso_(microprocessor)" title="Espresso (microprocessor)">Espresso</a>" <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">central processing unit</a> (CPU) and a "Latte" <a href="/wiki/Graphics_processing_unit" title="Graphics processing unit">graphics chip</a> (GPU), as well as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SEEPROM" title="SEEPROM">SEEPROM</a> memory chip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iwata_Asks:_Wii_U_1_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iwata_Asks:_Wii_U_1-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fail0verflow_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-fail0verflow-78">[78]</a></sup> The Espresso CPU, designed by IBM, consists of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PowerPC_750" title="PowerPC 750">PowerPC 750</a>-based tri-core processor with 3¬†MB of shared <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L2_cache" title="L2 cache">L2 cache</a> memory and clocked at approximately 1.24¬†GHz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Official_specs_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Official_specs-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clock_speeds_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-clock_speeds-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unconfirmed_hardware_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Unconfirmed_hardware-81">[note 1]</a></sup> Despite belonging to the <a href="/wiki/PowerPC" title="PowerPC">PowerPC</a> family, the Espresso also shares some architectural concepts with the <a href="/wiki/POWER7" title="POWER7">POWER7</a> architecture, such as the use of <a href="/wiki/EDRAM" title="EDRAM">eDRAM</a> cache and being manufactured at a <a href="/wiki/45_nanometer" title="45 nanometer">45¬†nm</a> node.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IBM_CPU_specs_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-IBM_CPU_specs-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unconfirmed_cache_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Unconfirmed_cache-84">[note 2]</a></sup> The Latte graphics chip contains both a "GX2" GPGPU, which runs Wii U applications, and a "GX" GPU, which enables backward compatibility with <a href="/wiki/Wii" title="Wii">Wii</a> games. The GX2, designed by AMD, is based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radeon_R600" title="Radeon R600">Radeon R600</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radeon_R700" title="Radeon R700">R700</a> architecture and is clocked at approximately 550¬†MHz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fail0verflow_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-fail0verflow-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clock_speeds_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-clock_speeds-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unconfirmed_hardware_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Unconfirmed_hardware-81">[note 1]</a></sup> It is manufactured at a 40¬†nm node and contains 32¬†MB of eDRAM cache memory, which can also act as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L3_cache" title="L3 cache">L3 cache</a> for the CPU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unconfirmed_hardware_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-Unconfirmed_hardware-81">[note 1]</a></sup> The GX, originally designed by <a href="/wiki/ATI_Technologies" title="ATI Technologies">ATI Technologies</a>, contains a 1¬†MB and a 2¬†MB banks of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESRAM" title="ESRAM">eSRAM</a> cache memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fail0verflow_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-fail0verflow-78">[78]</a></sup> The Latte chip also includes a secondary custom <a href="/wiki/ARM9" title="ARM9">ARM9</a> processor with 96¬†KB of SRAM memory that handles system tasks in the background during gameplay or while the system is in <a href="/wiki/Sleep_mode" title="Sleep mode">sleep mode</a>, and dedicated hardware audio <a href="/wiki/Digital_signal_processor" title="Digital signal processor">DSP</a> module.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fail0verflow_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-fail0verflow-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>The console contains 2¬†<a href="/wiki/Gigabyte" title="Gigabyte">GB</a> of <a href="/wiki/DDR3_SDRAM" title="DDR3 SDRAM">DDR3</a> system memory consisting of four 512¬†MB (4¬†<a href="/wiki/Gigabit" title="Gigabit">Gb</a>) <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_random-access_memory" title="Dynamic random-access memory">DRAM</a> chips with a maximum bandwidth of 12.8¬†GB/s. This is 20 times the amount found in the <a href="/wiki/Wii" title="Wii">Wii</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> Of this, 1¬†GB is reserved for the operating system and is unavailable to games. The memory architecture allows the CPU and GPU to access both the main DDR3 memory pool and the eDRAM cache memory pool on the GPU, removing the need for separate, dedicated memory pools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> The console includes either an 8¬†GB (Basic) or 32¬†GB (Premium <small>(WW)</small> / Deluxe <small>(NA)</small>) internal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EMMC" title="EMMC">eMMC</a> <a href="/wiki/Flash_memory" title="Flash memory">flash memory</a>, expandable via <a href="/wiki/Secure_Digital" title="Secure Digital">SD</a> memory cards up to 32¬†GB and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Serial_Bus" title="Universal Serial Bus">USB</a> external <a href="/wiki/Hard_disk_drive" title="Hard disk drive">hard disk drives</a> up to 2¬†TB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired_japan_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-wired_japan-88">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Wii U features <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/802.11_b/g" title="802.11 b/g">802.11 b/g</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/802.11_n" title="802.11 n">/n</a> <a href="/wiki/Wireless_network" title="Wireless network">wireless network</a> connectivity and support for <a href="/wiki/Fast_Ethernet" title="Fast Ethernet">Fast Ethernet</a> with an accessory, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bluetooth_4.0" title="Bluetooth 4.0">Bluetooth 4.0</a>, a total of four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USB_2.0" title="USB 2.0">USB 2.0</a> ports, and an SD/<a href="/wiki/Secure_Digital" title="Secure Digital">SDHC</a> memory card slot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bluetooth_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-bluetooth-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> An additional power port is also included to power the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wii_Sensor_Bar" title="Wii Sensor Bar">Wii Sensor Bar</a>, an auxiliary infrared emitter used by <a href="/wiki/Wii_Remote" title="Wii Remote">Wii Remote</a> peripherals for motion tracking. Video output options include <a href="/wiki/1080p" title="1080p">1080p</a>, <a href="/wiki/1080i" title="1080i">1080i</a>, <a href="/wiki/720p" title="720p">720p</a>, <a href="/wiki/576i" title="576i">576i</a>, <a href="/wiki/480p" title="480p">480p</a> and <a href="/wiki/480i" title="480i">480i</a>, through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HDMI_1.4" title="HDMI 1.4">HDMI 1.4</a> and <a href="/wiki/Component_video" title="Component video">component video</a> (<a href="/wiki/YPbPr" title="YPbPr">YP<sub>B</sub>P<sub>R</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/D-Terminal" title="D-Terminal">D-Terminal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RGBS" title="RGBS">RGB</a> <a href="/wiki/SCART" title="SCART">SCART</a>) or 576i, 480i <a href="/wiki/Anamorphic_widescreen" title="Anamorphic widescreen">anamorphic widescreen</a> through <a href="/wiki/Composite_video" title="Composite video">composite video</a> <small>(<a href="/wiki/S-Video" title="S-Video">S-Video</a>, <a href="/wiki/SCART" title="SCART">SCART</a> and D-Terminal)</small>. Audio output options include six-channel <a href="/wiki/5.1_surround_sound" title="5.1 surround sound">5.1</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_pulse-code_modulation" title="Linear pulse-code modulation">linear PCM</a> surround sound or <a href="/wiki/Stereophonic_sound" title="Stereophonic sound">analog stereo</a>. The console also supports <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopy" title="Stereoscopy">stereoscopic</a> (3D) images and video.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WiiU3D_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-WiiU3D-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Official_specs_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Official_specs-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controllers">Controllers</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wii_U_controller_illustration.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="353" data-file-width="600" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Wii_U_controller_illustration.svg/220px-Wii_U_controller_illustration.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Wii_U_controller_illustration.svg/330px-Wii_U_controller_illustration.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Wii_U_controller_illustration.svg/440px-Wii_U_controller_illustration.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wii_U_controller_illustration.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An illustration of the Wii U GamePad (White)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wii_U_GamePad" title="Wii U GamePad">Wii U GamePad</a> is the console's primary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_game_controller" title="Video game controller">controller</a>: its main feature is a built-in 6.2¬†inch (15.7¬†cm) <a href="/wiki/Resistive_touchscreen" title="Resistive touchscreen">resistive touchscreen</a>, which can be used as a <a href="/wiki/Second_screen" title="Second screen">companion</a> to games being played on a television, or as a means of playing games <a href="/wiki/Off-TV_Play" title="Off-TV Play">on the GamePad itself</a> without a television. The GamePad is designed to enable a concept referred to by Nintendo as "<a href="/wiki/Asymmetry" title="Asymmetry">asymmetric</a> gaming": in <a href="/wiki/Multiplayer_video_game" title="Multiplayer video game">multiplayer</a> games, a player using the GamePad may have a different gameplay objective and experience than other players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamasutra-gamepad_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamasutra-gamepad-90">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metro-nl_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-metro-nl-91">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>The GamePad's display contents are rendered on the Wii U itself, and streamed wirelessly as video to the GamePad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crecente1_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-crecente1-92">[90]</a></sup> The GamePad also supports <a href="/wiki/Near_field_communication" title="Near field communication">near field communications</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup> cards and specially-designed figurines, such as Nintendo's <a href="/wiki/Amiibo" title="Amiibo">Amiibo</a> line, can be used with the GamePad to interact with games. In Japan, it can also be used for <a href="/wiki/Contactless_payment" title="Contactless payment">contactless payments</a> from eShop with <a href="/wiki/Suica" title="Suica">Suica</a> cards. NFC payment will be made available for other regions in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polygon-suica_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-polygon-suica-94">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Briefing_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Briefing-96">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wii_U_Pro_Controller.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="852" data-file-width="1280" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Wii_U_Pro_Controller.JPG/220px-Wii_U_Pro_Controller.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Wii_U_Pro_Controller.JPG/330px-Wii_U_Pro_Controller.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Wii_U_Pro_Controller.JPG/440px-Wii_U_Pro_Controller.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wii_U_Pro_Controller.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A white Wii U Pro Controller</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A new <a href="/wiki/Wii_U_Pro_Controller" title="Wii U Pro Controller">Wii U Pro Controller</a> was released alongside the Wii U. The Wii U Pro Controller is an updated version of the Wii's <a href="/wiki/Classic_Controller" title="Classic Controller">Classic Controller</a> that is designed to appeal to "<a href="/wiki/Gamer#Hardcore_gamer" title="Gamer">hardcore</a>" players, with a more traditional <a href="/wiki/Gamepad" title="Gamepad">gamepad</a> design that resembles those used by the <a href="/wiki/PlayStation" title="PlayStation">PlayStation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xbox" title="Xbox">Xbox</a> lines (and in particular, the <a href="/wiki/Xbox_360" title="Xbox 360">Xbox 360</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xbox_360_Controller" title="Xbox 360 Controller">controller</a>), and a claimed 80-hour battery life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup> The Wii U Pro Controller is compatible with <a href="/wiki/List_of_Wii_U_games_that_use_the_Wii_U_Pro_Controller" title="List of Wii U games that use the Wii U Pro Controller">supported Wii U games</a>, but is not supported by Wii games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Wii U is compatible with existing <a href="/wiki/Wii_Remote" title="Wii Remote">Wii Remote</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wii_Remote_Plus" title="Wii Remote Plus">Wii Remote Plus</a> controllers, along with their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wii_Nunchuk" title="Wii Nunchuk">Nunchuk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classic_Controller" title="Classic Controller">Classic Controller</a> attachments. A combination of up to four Wii Remotes or Pro Controllers can be used simultaneously, and the console can theoretically support up to two GamePads. Most software requires a Wii Sensor Bar in order to use Wii Remotes with the system, though some aspects, such as Off-TV Play and the Wii Mode, allow the Wii U GamePad to detect Wii Remotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup> The Wii U does not natively support <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nintendo_GameCube_controller" title="Nintendo GameCube controller">Nintendo GameCube controllers</a>. While a USB adapter accessory allows GameCube controllers to be used on the console in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super_Smash_Bros_for_Nintendo_3DS_and_Wii_U" title="Super Smash Bros for Nintendo 3DS and Wii U">Super Smash Bros. for Wii U</a></i>, it is not compatible with any other software.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurogamer-gcwiiu_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-eurogamer-gcwiiu-106">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GameCube_Controller_and_SSB_at_E3_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-GameCube_Controller_and_SSB_at_E3-107">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Software">Software</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wii_U_system_software" title="Wii U system software">Wii U system software</a></div>
<p>The Wii U's main menu has two main components: by default, the GamePad displays a home screen consisting of a grid with shortcuts to games and apps, while the television screen displays a lobby (WaraWara Plaza) populated by other users' <a href="/wiki/Mii" title="Mii">Miis</a>. The two displays can be switched between the television screen and GamePad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge-onlinewarawara_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge-onlinewarawara-108">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polygon-warawara_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-polygon-warawara-109">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pressing the controller's "Home" button suspends the current game or app and opens the Home Menu: it shows basic information (such as the current time, controller battery levels, and notifications), and allows access to several "multitasking" functions, including the <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_eShop" title="Nintendo eShop">Nintendo eShop</a>, Miiverse, download manager, a web browser,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup> and the user's friends list. To play <a href="/wiki/Wii" title="Wii">Wii</a> games, the user must enter "Wii Mode", a <a href="/wiki/Legacy_mode" title="Legacy mode">legacy mode</a> that emulates the Wii's system software and <a href="/wiki/Wii_Menu" title="Wii Menu">Wii Menu</a> interface. Initially, Wii Mode could only be used on the television screen, but the October 2013 firmware update enables Wii Mode to be used off-TV. Either way, Wii controllers must be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time-wiiofftv_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-time-wiiofftv-112">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Online_services">Online services</span></h3>
<p>The Wii U uses the <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Network" title="Nintendo Network">Nintendo Network</a> platform for online services (replacing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friend_code" title="Friend code">friend code</a> system of the Wii), enabling online multiplayer, downloading and purchasing games or apps via Nintendo eShop, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_chat" title="Video chat">video chat</a> using the GamePad's camera and the <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Network#Wii_U_Chat" title="Nintendo Network">Wii U Chat</a> service, and other services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iw-videochat_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-iw-videochat-113">[111]</a></sup> Up to twelve accounts can be created per console.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eg-nnetwork12_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-eg-nnetwork12-114">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Social_networking_service" title="Social networking service">social networking service</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Network#Miiverse" title="Nintendo Network">Miiverse</a> is integrated into the Wii U's system software; it allows players to interact and share content in game-specific communities using their <a href="/wiki/Mii" title="Mii">Mii</a> as an avatar. Miiverse allows users to share accomplishments, screenshots, drawings and hand-written notes. Select games are integrated with Miiverse, where social interactions can also occur within the game, or appear within their content (such as in <i><a href="/wiki/Splatoon" title="Splatoon">Splatoon</a></i>, where drawn posts can appear as <a href="/wiki/Graffiti" title="Graffiti">graffiti</a> on walls in its settings).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge-onlinewarawara_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-verge-onlinewarawara-108">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polygon-warawara_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-polygon-warawara-109">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-avclub-splatoonmiiverse_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-avclub-splatoonmiiverse-115">[113]</a></sup> Miiverse is moderated through software filtering as well as a <a href="/wiki/Human_resources" title="Human resources">human resource</a> team in order to ensure that the content shared by users is appropriate and that no <a href="/wiki/Spoiler_(media)" title="Spoiler (media)">spoilers</a> are shared. In order to facilitate this, it was initially stated that comments posted could take up to 30 minutes to appear on Miiverse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multimedia_integration">Multimedia integration</span></h3>
<p>The Wii U supports online video services through apps, such as <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Video" title="Amazon Video">Amazon Video</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crunchyroll" title="Crunchyroll">Crunchyroll</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hulu" title="Hulu">Hulu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netflix" title="Netflix">Netflix</a>, and <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polygon-tviidiscontinued_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-polygon-tviidiscontinued-117">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup> The Wii U does not support playback of <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">DVD</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blu-ray_Disc" title="Blu-ray Disc">Blu-ray Disc</a>; Iwata explained that the decision to exclude these formats was motivated primarily by patent licensing fees, and the fact that such functionality would be redundant to DVD and Blu-ray players that users may already own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-engadget-nodvd_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-engadget-nodvd-119">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Network#Nintendo_TVii" title="Nintendo Network">Nintendo TVii</a> service allowed program listings from a user's television provider to be aggregated with online video on demand services into an <a href="/wiki/Electronic_program_guide" title="Electronic program guide">electronic program guide</a> with recommendations and search functionality, and the ability to use the GamePad's infrared functionality to tune a <a href="/wiki/Set-top_box" title="Set-top box">set-top box</a> to a particular program. The app also provided integration with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IMDB" title="IMDB">IMDB</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rotten_Tomatoes" title="Rotten Tomatoes">Rotten Tomatoes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia" title="Wikipedia">Wikipedia</a> to provide additional information, and social connectivity through <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a>, <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a>, or Miiverse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ign-tvii_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-ign-tvii-120">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup> TVii was made available with the Wii U's release in Japan on December 8, 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup> and in North America on December 20, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup> Following delays, a European launch was cancelled,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gameinformer-notviieurope_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-gameinformer-notviieurope-124">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techradar-tviiuk_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-techradar-tviiuk-125">[123]</a></sup> and the service was discontinued in North America on August 11, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polygon-tviidiscontinued_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-polygon-tviidiscontinued-117">[115]</a></sup> The service remains active in Japan only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Games">Games</span></h2>
<p><span id="Wii_U_Optical_Disc"></span></p>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Wii_U_software" title="List of Wii U software">List of Wii U software</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_Wii_U_video_games" title="List of best-selling Wii U video games">List of best-selling Wii U video games</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wii_U_game_case.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="800" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Wii_U_game_case.png/220px-Wii_U_game_case.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Wii_U_game_case.png/330px-Wii_U_game_case.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Wii_U_game_case.png/440px-Wii_U_game_case.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wii_U_game_case.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An opened <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wii_U_Optical_Disc" title="Wii U Optical Disc">Wii U Optical Disc</a> case, without its paper cover and instructions. The eco-friendly design uses less plastic, reducing manufacturing waste. The Recycle logo does not appear on European or Japanese cases. These cases are identical to common Wii cases made for Nintendo-published titles released since circa 2010, save for having Nintendo's own logo embossed above the disc area instead of Wii's logo.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wii U games can be downloaded digitally through <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_eShop" title="Nintendo eShop">Nintendo eShop</a>, or at retail on physical media. Retail copies of Wii U games are distributed on Wii U Optical Disc, a proprietary <a href="/wiki/High-density_storage_media" title="High-density storage media">high-density</a> <a href="/wiki/Optical_disc" title="Optical disc">optical disc</a> format co-developed with <a href="/wiki/Panasonic" title="Panasonic">Panasonic</a>. The format is similar in design and specifications to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blu-ray_Disc" title="Blu-ray Disc">Blu-ray Disc</a>, with a capacity of 25¬†<a href="/wiki/Gigabyte" title="Gigabyte">GB</a> per layer, but the discs themselves have a soft, rounded rim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup> Unlike previous Nintendo consoles, game manuals are only available digitally. The console is <a href="/wiki/Regional_lockout" title="Regional lockout">region locked</a>; software purchased in a region can be only played on that region's hardware.</p>
<p>New games in Nintendo's flagship franchises (including <a href="/wiki/Super_Mario" title="Super Mario">Super Mario</a>, <a href="/wiki/Donkey_Kong" title="Donkey Kong">Donkey Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Legend_of_Zelda" title="The Legend of Zelda">The Legend of Zelda</a>), as well as several <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wii_(series)" title="Wii (series)">Wii series</a></i> games (including <i><a href="/wiki/Wii_Sports_Club" title="Wii Sports Club">Wii Sports Club</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Wii_Fit_U" title="Wii Fit U">Wii Fit U</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Wii_Party_U" title="Wii Party U">Wii Party U</a></i>) have been released, in addition to many original titles and third-party-developed games. Nintendo has received third-party support from companies such as <a href="/wiki/Ubisoft" title="Ubisoft">Ubisoft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sega" title="Sega">Sega</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Interactive_Entertainment" title="Warner Bros. Interactive Entertainment">Warner Bros. Interactive Entertainment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Activision_Blizzard" title="Activision Blizzard">Activision Blizzard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Capcom" title="Capcom">Capcom</a>, and various independent developers such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two_Tribes_B.V." title="Two Tribes B.V.">Two Tribes</a>. As of late July 2016, there have been 39 first-party and 118 third-party Wii U games physically released in the United States; both figures are the lowest for any Nintendo console.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>A total of 92.35 million Wii U games have been sold worldwide as of September 30, 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Wii_U&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hardware_Software_Sales_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hardware_Software_Sales-3">[3]</a></sup> with ten titles surpassing the million-unit mark. The highest selling game is <i><a href="/wiki/Mario_Kart_8" title="Mario Kart 8">Mario Kart 8</a></i> at 8 million units, followed by <i><a href="/wiki/New_Super_Mario_Bros._U" title="New Super Mario Bros. U">New Super Mario Bros. U</a></i> at 5.45 million units, and <i><a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Land" title="Nintendo Land">Nintendo Land</a></i> at 5.13 million units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wii_U_best-selling_games_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wii_U_best-selling_games-5">[5]</a></sup> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super_Smash_Bros._for_Wii_U" title="Super Smash Bros. for Wii U">Super Smash Bros. for Wii U</a></i> is the fastest selling Wii U game as of November 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Wii_U&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cvg-smashwiiusales_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-cvg-smashwiiusales-131">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_titles">Launch titles</span></h3>
<p>The Wii U was launched with 23 games in North America,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup> 26 games in Europe, 25 games in Australia, and 11 games in Japan. Some download-only games were also available on launch day for the Wii U via <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_eShop" title="Nintendo eShop">Nintendo eShop</a>. An additional 30 games were announced for release during the system's launch window, which included the three months after the system's launch date.</p>

</ul>

<td><sup>NA, PAL</sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virtual_Console">Virtual Console</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Virtual_Console" title="Virtual Console">Virtual Console</a></div>
<p>In January 2013, Nintendo announced that <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_Entertainment_System" title="Nintendo Entertainment System">NES</a> and <a href="/wiki/Super_Nintendo_Entertainment_System" title="Super Nintendo Entertainment System">Super NES</a> titles would be made available for the <b><a href="/wiki/Virtual_Console" title="Virtual Console">Virtual Console</a></b> service on the Wii U in April 2013 and would include the option to use <a href="/wiki/Off-TV_Play" title="Off-TV Play">Off-TV Play</a> on the GamePad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup> On March 26, 2014, <a href="/wiki/Game_Boy_Advance" title="Game Boy Advance">Game Boy Advance</a> titles were confirmed and started to appear on the eShop the following month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_64" title="Nintendo 64">Nintendo 64</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_DS" title="Nintendo DS">Nintendo DS</a> games were added in April 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Backward_compatibility">Backward compatibility</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Wii_games" title="List of Wii games">List of Wii games</a></div>
<p>The Wii U is compatible with all <a href="/wiki/Wii" title="Wii">Wii</a> games and Wii accessories such as the <a href="/wiki/Wii_Remote" title="Wii Remote">Wii Remote</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wii_Remote_Plus" title="Wii Remote Plus">Plus</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wii_Nunchuk" title="Wii Nunchuk">Wii Nunchuk</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Wii_Balance_Board" title="Wii Balance Board">Wii Balance Board</a>. It is possible to migrate most downloaded software and save files from a Wii to a Wii U.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired_japan_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-wired_japan-88">[86]</a></sup> Although Wii games can be displayed on the GamePad's screen, they can only be controlled using a Wii controller, and not the GamePad's controller.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time-wiiofftv_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-time-wiiofftv-112">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Wii U is not compatible with <a href="/wiki/GameCube" title="GameCube">GameCube</a> discs or accessories. A USB <a href="/wiki/GameCube_controller" title="GameCube controller">GameCube controller</a> adapter with four ports was released exclusively for use with <i><a href="/wiki/Super_Smash_Bros._for_Nintendo_3DS_and_Wii_U" title="Super Smash Bros. for Nintendo 3DS and Wii U">Super Smash Bros. for Wii U</a></i>, and does not support any other titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurogamer-gcwiiu_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-eurogamer-gcwiiu-106">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GameCube_Controller_and_SSB_at_E3_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-GameCube_Controller_and_SSB_at_E3-107">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Developers">Developers</span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Wii_U&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(December 2016)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The Nintendo Web Framework, unveiled in 2013, is a toolset that uses modern web technology (<a href="/wiki/WebKit" title="WebKit">WebKit</a>, <a href="/wiki/HTML5" title="HTML5">HTML5</a>, and <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a>) to simplify the process of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porting_of_video_games" title="Porting of video games">porting</a> games to the Wii U's GamePad and TV. It was designed to make web-based games easier to port to the system than it would to code specifically for the Wii U.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span></h2>
<p>John Teti of <a href="/wiki/The_A.V._Club" title="The A.V. Club">The A.V. Club</a>'s Gameological Society considers the Wii U a compelling video game system which lacks focus, citing <i>Nintendo Land</i> as "ideas act[ing] in service of the technology".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-avclub_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-avclub-139">[137]</a></sup> Ben Gilbert of <a href="/wiki/Engadget" title="Engadget">Engadget</a> states that Nintendo delivers on its promise of releasing "a modern HD gaming console" but notes that "there are also some major missteps and half-baked ideas: a befuddling Friends List/Miiverse connection, a complete lack of many system-wide console standards (group chat, achievements, the ability to play non-game disc-based media) and a game controller that lasts only 3.5 hours", and stated that he could not give a complete assessment of the console with online components such as Nintendo TVii missing at launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-engadget_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-engadget-140">[138]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/TechRadar" title="TechRadar">TechRadar</a> praised the system's GamePad functionality and HD graphics, but criticized the limited battery power on the GamePad, and the insufficient number of top-tier launch titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup> Some industry figures do not consider the Wii U as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_video_game_consoles_(eighth_generation)" title="History of video game consoles (eighth generation)">eighth-generation</a> console,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup> with many citing the hardware's processing speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup> However, Fils-Aim√© has noted that similar comments were made in 2006 when the <a href="/wiki/Wii" title="Wii">Wii</a> first launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the launch of other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_video_game_consoles_(eighth_generation)" title="History of video game consoles (eighth generation)">eighth-generation</a> consoles, the <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_4" title="PlayStation 4">PlayStation 4</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xbox_One" title="Xbox One">Xbox One</a>, in November 2013, some critics suggested that the Wii U would continue to struggle as it lacked the third-party support of its rivals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup> In December 2013 article, Chris Suellentrop of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> argued that the Wii U was the only new console with a video game worth playing‚Äî<i><a href="/wiki/Super_Mario_3D_World" title="Super Mario 3D World">Super Mario 3D World</a></i>‚Äîwhich he described as being "the best <i>Mario</i> game in years". Despite the praise, he noted that "one great game won't save a console", and although other good games exist on the Wii U, he admitted that its lineup "[was] still pretty thin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a> writer Matt Peckham said that the Wii U was the system of choice to pick up during that Christmas season, praising the console's game lineup, affordable price, Off-TV Play, the absence of subscription fees for its online services, <a href="/wiki/Backward_compatibility" title="Backward compatibility">backward compatibility</a> and media features. However, he noted that the system still needs a price cut and an improved first and third-party software lineup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[147]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/CNET" title="CNET">CNET</a> also noted that the Wii U had a better lineup of games and lower price in comparison to its competitors, mainly due to its one-year head start.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup></p>
